</dl>
<p>This can be verified by showing that it has the same value at <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i> = 1</span>, and the same derivative.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transcendence_of_the_logarithm">Transcendence of the logarithm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Transcendence of the logarithm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">Real numbers</a> that are not <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_number" title="Algebraic number">algebraic</a> are called <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_number" title="Transcendental number">transcendental</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup> for example, <a href="/wiki/Pi" title="Pi"><span class="texhtml">π</span></a> and <i><a href="/wiki/E_(mathematical_constant)" title="E (mathematical constant)">e</a></i> are such numbers, but <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mn>2</mn>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mn>3</mn>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\sqrt {2-{\sqrt {3}}}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\sqrt {2-{\sqrt {3}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/75b2a724c326f7f59f71baa9788cf455a0609bdc" style="vertical-align: -1.671ex; width:9.477ex; height:4.843ex;"/></span> is not. <a href="/wiki/Almost_all" title="Almost all">Almost all</a> real numbers are transcendental. The logarithm is an example of a <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_function" title="Transcendental function">transcendental function</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Gelfond%E2%80%93Schneider_theorem" title="Gelfond–Schneider theorem">Gelfond–Schneider theorem</a> asserts that logarithms usually take transcendental, i.e., "difficult" values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Calculation">Calculation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Calculation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Logarithm_keys.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1411" data-file-width="1882" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Logarithm_keys.jpg/220px-Logarithm_keys.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Logarithm_keys.jpg/330px-Logarithm_keys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Logarithm_keys.jpg/440px-Logarithm_keys.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Logarithm_keys.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The logarithm keys (<i>lo</i>g for base-10 and <i>ln</i> for base-<i>e</i>) on a typical scientific calculator</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Logarithms are easy to compute in some cases, such as <span class="texhtml">log<sub>10</sub>(1000) = 3</span>. In general, logarithms can be calculated using <a href="/wiki/Power_series" title="Power series">power series</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic%E2%80%93geometric_mean" title="Arithmetic–geometric mean">arithmetic–geometric mean</a>, or be retrieved from a precalculated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logarithm_table" title="Logarithm table">logarithm table</a> that provides a fixed precision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_method" title="Newton's method">Newton's method</a>, an iterative method to solve equations approximately, can also be used to calculate the logarithm, because its inverse function, the exponential function, can be computed efficiently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup> Using look-up tables, <a href="/wiki/CORDIC" title="CORDIC">CORDIC</a>-like methods can be used to compute logarithms if the only available operations are addition and <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic_shift" title="Arithmetic shift">bit shifts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup> Moreover, the <a href="/wiki/Binary_logarithm#Algorithm" title="Binary logarithm">binary logarithm algorithm</a> calculates <span class="texhtml">lb(<i>x</i>)</span> <a href="/wiki/Recursion" title="Recursion">recursively</a> based on repeated squarings of <i>x</i>, taking advantage of the relation</p>
<dl>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_series">Power series</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Power series">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl>
<dt>Taylor series</dt>
</dl>

<p>For example, with <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = 1.5</span> the third approximation yields 0.4167, which is about 0.011 greater than <span class="texhtml">ln(1.5) = 0.405465</span>. This <a href="/wiki/Series_(mathematics)" title="Series (mathematics)">series</a> approximates ln(<i>z</i>) with arbitrary precision, provided the number of summands is large enough. In elementary calculus, <span class="texhtml">ln(<i>z</i>)</span> is therefore the <a href="/wiki/Limit_(mathematics)" title="Limit (mathematics)"><i>limit</i></a> of this series. It is the <a href="/wiki/Taylor_series" title="Taylor series">Taylor series</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">natural logarithm</a> at <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = 1</span>. The Taylor series of ln <i>z</i> provides a particularly useful approximation to <span class="texhtml">ln(1+<i>z</i>)</span> when <i>z</i> is small, <span class="texhtml">|<i>z</i>| &lt; 1</span>, since then</p>

<mi>z</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \ln(1+z)=z-{\frac {z^{2}}{2}}+{\frac {z^{3}}{3}}\cdots \approx z.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\ln(1+z)=z-{\frac {z^{2}}{2}}+{\frac {z^{3}}{3}}\cdots \approx z." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e89327f36f52ef490f4bf487c232afc707e4bfb" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:32.805ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>For example, with <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = 0.1</span> the first-order approximation gives <span class="texhtml">ln(1.1) ≈ 0.1</span>, which is less than 5% off the correct value 0.0953.</p>

<p>This series can be derived from the above Taylor series. It converges more quickly than the Taylor series, especially if <i>z</i> is close to 1. For example, for {<span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = 1.5</span>, the first three terms of the second series approximate <span class="texhtml">ln(1.5)</span> with an error of about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6994300000000000000♠</span>3<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−6</sup></span>. The quick convergence for <i>z</i> close to 1 can be taken advantage of in the following way: given a low-accuracy approximation <span class="texhtml"><i>y</i> ≈ ln(<i>z</i>)</span> and putting</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>A</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>z</mi>
<mrow>
<mi>exp</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>y</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle A={\frac {z}{\exp(y)}},\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="A={\frac {z}{\exp(y)}},\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d064e79eb939fa056d0f36427ef88321f399468f" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:13.323ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>the logarithm of <i>z</i> is:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>z</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>y</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>A</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>.</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \ln(z)=y+\ln(A).\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\ln(z)=y+\ln(A).\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b93e2feb29b2cad38548a80098725d9a1b571267" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:18.603ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The better the initial approximation <i>y</i> is, the closer <i>A</i> is to 1, so its logarithm can be calculated efficiently. <i>A</i> can be calculated using the <a href="/wiki/Exponential_function" title="Exponential function">exponential series</a>, which converges quickly provided <i>y</i> is not too large. Calculating the logarithm of larger <i>z</i> can be reduced to smaller values of <i>z</i> by writing <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = <i>a</i> · 10<sup><i>b</i></sup></span>, so that <span class="texhtml">ln(<i>z</i>) = ln(<i>a</i>) + <i>b</i> · ln(10)</span>.</p>
<p>A closely related method can be used to compute the logarithm of integers. From the above series, it follows that:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mi>n</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>+</mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<munderover>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>k</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">∞<!-- ∞ --></mi>
</mrow>
</munderover>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>k</mi>
<mo>+</mo>

<mo>+</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \ln(n+1)=\ln(n)+2\sum _{k=0}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{2k+1}}\left({\frac {1}{2n+1}}\right)^{2k+1}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\ln(n+1)=\ln(n)+2\sum _{k=0}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{2k+1}}\left({\frac {1}{2n+1}}\right)^{2k+1}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/306cedcb8ef32fe57d535b3c27b2ae6af9b13326" style="vertical-align: -3.171ex; width:48.52ex; height:7.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>If the logarithm of a large integer <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">n</span> is known, then this series yields a fast converging series for <span class="texhtml">log(<i>n</i>+1)</span>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arithmetic.E2.80.93geometric_mean_approximation">Arithmetic–geometric mean approximation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Arithmetic–geometric mean approximation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic%E2%80%93geometric_mean" title="Arithmetic–geometric mean">arithmetic–geometric mean</a> yields high precision approximations of the <a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">natural logarithm</a>. <span class="texhtml">ln(<i>x</i>)</span> is approximated to a precision of <span class="texhtml">2<sup>−<i>p</i></sup></span> (or <i>p</i> precise bits) by the following formula (due to <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a>):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[52]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>≈<!-- ≈ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>M</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
<mo>,</mo>
<msup>
<mn>2</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>m</mi>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \ln(x)\approx {\frac {\pi }{2M(1,2^{2-m}/x)}}-m\ln(2).}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\ln(x)\approx {\frac {\pi }{2M(1,2^{2-m}/x)}}-m\ln(2)." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f4f0044fb2bdba6bee5dcc5b57ac9fc62a5edbb9" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:35.142ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Here <span class="texhtml">M(<i>x</i>,<i>y</i>)</span> denotes the <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic%E2%80%93geometric_mean" title="Arithmetic–geometric mean">arithmetic–geometric mean</a> of <i>x</i> and <i>y</i>. It is obtained by repeatedly calculating the average <span class="texhtml">(x+y)/2</span> (<a href="/wiki/Arithmetic_mean" title="Arithmetic mean">arithmetic mean</a>) and <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mi>x</mi>
<mi>y</mi>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\sqrt {xy}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\sqrt  {xy}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1ba7940afda51223b04f0738981d07c17b1830e8" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:4.452ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> (<a href="/wiki/Geometric_mean" title="Geometric mean">geometric mean</a>) of <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> then let those two numbers become the next <i>x</i> and <i>y</i>. The two numbers quickly converge to a common limit which is the value of <span class="texhtml">M(<i>x</i>,<i>y</i>)</span>. <i>m</i> is chosen such that</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<msup>
<mn>2</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>&gt;</mo>
<msup>
<mn>2</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>p</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>.</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x\,2^{m}&gt;2^{p/2}.\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x\,2^{m}&gt;2^{p/2}.\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/80443424cc40c061dba53d32f86d2d8169aa1983" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:12.634ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>to insure the required precision. A larger <i>m</i> makes the <span class="texhtml">M(<i>x</i>,<i>y</i>)</span> calculation take more steps (the initial x and y are farther apart so it takes more steps to converge) but gives more precision. The constants <span class="texhtml">pi</span> and <span class="texhtml">ln(2)</span> can be calculated with quickly converging series.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feynman.27s_algorithm">Feynman's algorithm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Feynman's algorithm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While at <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a> working on the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Feynman" title="Richard Feynman">Richard Feynman</a> developed a bit processing algorithm that is similar to long division and was later used in the <a href="/wiki/Connection_Machine" title="Connection Machine">Connection Machine</a>. The algorithm uses the fact that every real number <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mn>1</mn>
<mo>&lt;</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>&lt;</mo>
<mn>2</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 1&lt;x&lt;2}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle 1&lt;x&lt;2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/190211a7c9a56fc1683a97fcca23fcaccbd0050b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:9.904ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is uniquely representable as a product of distinct factors of the form <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mn>1</mn>
<mo>+</mo>
<msup>
<mn>2</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>k</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 1+2^{-k}}</annotation>
</semantics>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NautilusCutawayLogarithmicSpiral.jpg"><img alt="A photograph of a nautilus' shell." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1693" data-file-width="2240" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/NautilusCutawayLogarithmicSpiral.jpg/220px-NautilusCutawayLogarithmicSpiral.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/NautilusCutawayLogarithmicSpiral.jpg/330px-NautilusCutawayLogarithmicSpiral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/NautilusCutawayLogarithmicSpiral.jpg/440px-NautilusCutawayLogarithmicSpiral.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NautilusCutawayLogarithmicSpiral.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Nautilus" title="Nautilus">nautilus</a> displaying a logarithmic spiral</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Logarithms have many applications inside and outside mathematics. Some of these occurrences are related to the notion of <a href="/wiki/Scale_invariance" title="Scale invariance">scale invariance</a>. For example, each chamber of the shell of a <a href="/wiki/Nautilus" title="Nautilus">nautilus</a> is an approximate copy of the next one, scaled by a constant factor. This gives rise to a <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_spiral" title="Logarithmic spiral">logarithmic spiral</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Benford%27s_law" title="Benford's law">Benford's law</a> on the distribution of leading digits can also be explained by scale invariance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[55]</a></sup> Logarithms are also linked to <a href="/wiki/Self-similarity" title="Self-similarity">self-similarity</a>. For example, logarithms appear in the analysis of algorithms that solve a problem by dividing it into two similar smaller problems and patching their solutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[56]</a></sup> The dimensions of self-similar geometric shapes, that is, shapes whose parts resemble the overall picture are also based on logarithms. <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_scale" title="Logarithmic scale">Logarithmic scales</a> are useful for quantifying the relative change of a value as opposed to its absolute difference. Moreover, because the logarithmic function <span class="texhtml">log(<i>x</i>)</span> grows very slowly for large <i>x</i>, logarithmic scales are used to compress large-scale scientific data. Logarithms also occur in numerous scientific formulas, such as the <a href="/wiki/Tsiolkovsky_rocket_equation" title="Tsiolkovsky rocket equation">Tsiolkovsky rocket equation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fenske_equation" title="Fenske equation">Fenske equation</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Nernst_equation" title="Nernst equation">Nernst equation</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Logarithmic_scale">Logarithmic scale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Logarithmic scale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_scale" title="Logarithmic scale">Logarithmic scale</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Germany_Hyperinflation.svg"><img alt="A graph of the value of one mark over time. The line showing its value is increasing very quickly, even with logarithmic scale." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="509" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Germany_Hyperinflation.svg/220px-Germany_Hyperinflation.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Germany_Hyperinflation.svg/330px-Germany_Hyperinflation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Germany_Hyperinflation.svg/440px-Germany_Hyperinflation.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Germany_Hyperinflation.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A logarithmic chart depicting the value of one <a href="/wiki/German_gold_mark" title="German gold mark">Goldmark</a> in <a href="/wiki/German_Papiermark" title="German Papiermark">Papiermarks</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflation_in_the_Weimar_Republic" title="Inflation in the Weimar Republic">German hyperinflation in the 1920s</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Scientific quantities are often expressed as logarithms of other quantities, using a <i>logarithmic scale</i>. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">decibel</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unit_of_measurement" title="Unit of measurement">unit of measurement</a> associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logarithmic-scale" title="Logarithmic-scale">logarithmic-scale</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Level_quantity" title="Level quantity">quantities</a>. It is based on the common logarithm of <a href="/wiki/Ratio" title="Ratio">ratios</a>—10 times the common logarithm of a <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">power</a> ratio or 20 times the common logarithm of a <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltage</a> ratio. It is used to quantify the loss of voltage levels in transmitting electrical signals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[57]</a></sup> to describe power levels of sounds in <a href="/wiki/Acoustics" title="Acoustics">acoustics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[58]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Absorbance" title="Absorbance">absorbance</a> of light in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">spectrometry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Signal-to-noise_ratio" title="Signal-to-noise ratio">signal-to-noise ratio</a> describing the amount of unwanted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noise_(electronic)" title="Noise (electronic)">noise</a> in relation to a (meaningful) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signal_(information_theory)" title="Signal (information theory)">signal</a> is also measured in decibels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[59]</a></sup> In a similar vein, the <a href="/wiki/Peak_signal-to-noise_ratio" title="Peak signal-to-noise ratio">peak signal-to-noise ratio</a> is commonly used to assess the quality of sound and <a href="/wiki/Image_compression" title="Image compression">image compression</a> methods using the logarithm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The strength of an earthquake is measured by taking the common logarithm of the energy emitted at the quake. This is used in the <a href="/wiki/Moment_magnitude_scale" title="Moment magnitude scale">moment magnitude scale</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Richter_magnitude_scale" title="Richter magnitude scale">Richter magnitude scale</a>. For example, a 5.0 earthquake releases 32 times <span class="texhtml">(10<sup>1.5</sup>)</span> and a 6.0 releases 1000 times <span class="texhtml">(10<sup>3</sup>)</span> the energy of a 4.0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[61]</a></sup> Another logarithmic scale is <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a>. It measures the brightness of stars logarithmically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[62]</a></sup> Yet another example is <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>; pH is the negative of the common logarithm of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Activity_(chemistry)" title="Activity (chemistry)">activity</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hydronium" title="Hydronium">hydronium</a> ions (the form <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br/></span></span> take in water).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[63]</a></sup> The activity of hydronium ions in neutral water is 10<sup>−7</sup> <a href="/wiki/Molar_concentration" title="Molar concentration">mol·L<sup>−1</sup></a>, hence a pH of 7. Vinegar typically has a pH of about 3. The difference of 4 corresponds to a ratio of 10<sup>4</sup> of the activity, that is, vinegar's hydronium ion activity is about <span class="texhtml">10<sup>−3</sup> mol·L<sup>−1</sup></span>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Semi-log_plot" title="Semi-log plot">Semilog</a> (log-linear) graphs use the logarithmic scale concept for visualization: one axis, typically the vertical one, is scaled logarithmically. For example, the chart at the right compresses the steep increase from 1 million to 1 trillion to the same space (on the vertical axis) as the increase from 1 to 1 million. In such graphs, <a href="/wiki/Exponential_function" title="Exponential function">exponential functions</a> of the form <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i>(<i>x</i>) = <i>a</i> · <i>b</i><sup><i>x</i></sup></span> appear as straight lines with <a href="/wiki/Slope" title="Slope">slope</a> equal to the logarithm of <i>b</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Log-log_plot" title="Log-log plot">Log-log</a> graphs scale both axes logarithmically, which causes functions of the form <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i>(<i>x</i>) = <i>a</i> · <i>x</i><sup><i>k</i></sup></span> to be depicted as straight lines with slope equal to the exponent <i>k</i>. This is applied in visualizing and analyzing <a href="/wiki/Power_law" title="Power law">power laws</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology">Psychology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Psychology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Logarithms occur in several laws describing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_perception" title="Human perception">human perception</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hick%27s_law" title="Hick's law">Hick's law</a> proposes a logarithmic relation between the time individuals take to choose an alternative and the number of choices they have.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fitts%27s_law" title="Fitts's law">Fitts's law</a> predicts that the time required to rapidly move to a target area is a logarithmic function of the distance to and the size of the target.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[68]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Psychophysics" title="Psychophysics">psychophysics</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Weber%E2%80%93Fechner_law" title="Weber–Fechner law">Weber–Fechner law</a> proposes a logarithmic relationship between <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_(psychology)" title="Stimulus (psychology)">stimulus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sensation_(psychology)" title="Sensation (psychology)">sensation</a> such as the actual vs. the perceived weight of an item a person is carrying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[69]</a></sup> (This "law", however, is less precise than more recent models, such as the <a href="/wiki/Stevens%27_power_law" title="Stevens' power law">Stevens' power law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[70]</a></sup>)</p>
<p>Psychological studies found that individuals with little mathematics education tend to estimate quantities logarithmically, that is, they position a number on an unmarked line according to its logarithm, so that 10 is positioned as close to 100 as 100 is to 1000. Increasing education shifts this to a linear estimate (positioning 1000 10x as far away) in some circumstances, while logarithms are used when the numbers to be plotted are difficult to plot linearly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Probability_theory_and_statistics">Probability theory and statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Probability theory and statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PDF-log_normal_distributions.svg"><img alt="Three asymmetric PDF curves" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="390" data-file-width="390" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/PDF-log_normal_distributions.svg/220px-PDF-log_normal_distributions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/PDF-log_normal_distributions.svg/330px-PDF-log_normal_distributions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/PDF-log_normal_distributions.svg/440px-PDF-log_normal_distributions.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PDF-log_normal_distributions.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Three <a href="/wiki/Probability_density_function" title="Probability density function">probability density functions</a> (PDF) of random variables with log-normal distributions. The location parameter <span class="texhtml">μ</span>, which is zero for all three of the PDFs shown, is the mean of the logarithm of the random variable, not the mean of the variable itself.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benfords_law_illustrated_by_world%27s_countries_population.png"><img alt="A bar chart and a superimposed second chart. The two differ slightly, but both decrease in a similar fashion." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="263" data-file-width="425" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Benfords_law_illustrated_by_world%27s_countries_population.png/220px-Benfords_law_illustrated_by_world%27s_countries_population.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Benfords_law_illustrated_by_world%27s_countries_population.png/330px-Benfords_law_illustrated_by_world%27s_countries_population.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0b/Benfords_law_illustrated_by_world%27s_countries_population.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benfords_law_illustrated_by_world%27s_countries_population.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Distribution of first digits (in %, red bars) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_population" title="List of countries by population">population of the 237 countries</a> of the world. Black dots indicate the distribution predicted by Benford's law.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Logarithms arise in <a href="/wiki/Probability_theory" title="Probability theory">probability theory</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_large_numbers" title="Law of large numbers">law of large numbers</a> dictates that, for a <a href="/wiki/Fair_coin" title="Fair coin">fair coin</a>, as the number of coin-tosses increases to infinity, the observed proportion of heads [[binomial distribution#Symmetric binomial distribution (<span class="texhtml">p = 0.5</span>)|approaches one-half]]. The fluctuations of this proportion about one-half are described by the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_iterated_logarithm" title="Law of the iterated logarithm">law of the iterated logarithm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Logarithms also occur in <a href="/wiki/Log-normal_distribution" title="Log-normal distribution">log-normal distributions</a>. When the logarithm of a <a href="/wiki/Random_variable" title="Random variable">random variable</a> has a <a href="/wiki/Normal_distribution" title="Normal distribution">normal distribution</a>, the variable is said to have a log-normal distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup> Log-normal distributions are encountered in many fields, wherever a variable is formed as the product of many independent positive random variables, for example in the study of turbulence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Logarithms are used for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maximum-likelihood_estimation" title="Maximum-likelihood estimation">maximum-likelihood estimation</a> of parametric <a href="/wiki/Statistical_model" title="Statistical model">statistical models</a>. For such a model, the <a href="/wiki/Likelihood_function" title="Likelihood function">likelihood function</a> depends on at least one <a href="/wiki/Parametric_model" title="Parametric model">parameter</a> that must be estimated. A maximum of the likelihood function occurs at the same parameter-value as a maximum of the logarithm of the likelihood (the "<i>log likelihood</i>"), because the logarithm is an increasing function. The log-likelihood is easier to maximize, especially for the multiplied likelihoods for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independence_(probability)" title="Independence (probability)">independent</a> random variables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Benford%27s_law" title="Benford's law">Benford's law</a> describes the occurrence of digits in many <a href="/wiki/Data_set" title="Data set">data sets</a>, such as heights of buildings. According to Benford's law, the probability that the first decimal-digit of an item in the data sample is <i>d</i> (from 1 to 9) equals <span class="texhtml">log<sub>10</sub>(<i>d</i> + 1) − log<sub>10</sub>(<i>d</i>)</span>, <i>regardless</i> of the unit of measurement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[77]</a></sup> Thus, about 30% of the data can be expected to have 1 as first digit, 18% start with 2, etc. Auditors examine deviations from Benford's law to detect fraudulent accounting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Computational_complexity">Computational complexity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Computational complexity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Analysis_of_algorithms" title="Analysis of algorithms">Analysis of algorithms</a> is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> that studies the <a href="/wiki/Time_complexity" title="Time complexity">performance</a> of <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithms</a> (computer programs solving a certain problem).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wegener_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wegener-84">[79]</a></sup> Logarithms are valuable for describing algorithms that <a href="/wiki/Divide_and_conquer_algorithm" title="Divide and conquer algorithm">divide a problem</a> into smaller ones, and join the solutions of the subproblems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>For example, to find a number in a sorted list, the <a href="/wiki/Binary_search_algorithm" title="Binary search algorithm">binary search algorithm</a> checks the middle entry and proceeds with the half before or after the middle entry if the number is still not found. This algorithm requires, on average, <span class="texhtml">log<sub>2</sub>(<i>N</i>)</span> comparisons, where <i>N</i> is the list's length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[81]</a></sup> Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Merge_sort" title="Merge sort">merge sort</a> algorithm sorts an unsorted list by dividing the list into halves and sorting these first before merging the results. Merge sort algorithms typically require a time <a href="/wiki/Big_O_notation" title="Big O notation">approximately proportional to</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>N</i> · log(<i>N</i>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[82]</a></sup> The base of the logarithm is not specified here, because the result only changes by a constant factor when another base is used. A constant factor is usually disregarded in the analysis of algorithms under the standard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uniform_cost_model" title="Uniform cost model">uniform cost model</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wegener20_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wegener20-88">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>A function <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i>(<i>x</i>)</span> is said to <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_growth" title="Logarithmic growth">grow logarithmically</a> if <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i>(<i>x</i>)</span> is (exactly or approximately) proportional to the logarithm of <i>x</i>. (Biological descriptions of organism growth, however, use this term for an exponential function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[84]</a></sup>) For example, any <a href="/wiki/Natural_number" title="Natural number">natural number</a> <i>N</i> can be represented in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_numeral_system" title="Binary numeral system">binary form</a> in no more than <span class="texhtml">log<sub>2</sub>(<i>N</i>) + 1</span> <a href="/wiki/Bit" title="Bit">bits</a>. In other words, the amount of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memory_(computing)" title="Memory (computing)">memory</a> needed to store <i>N</i> grows logarithmically with <i>N</i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entropy_and_chaos">Entropy and chaos</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Entropy and chaos">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chaotic_Bunimovich_stadium.png"><img alt="An oval shape with the trajectories of two particles." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="379" data-file-width="758" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Chaotic_Bunimovich_stadium.png/220px-Chaotic_Bunimovich_stadium.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Chaotic_Bunimovich_stadium.png/330px-Chaotic_Bunimovich_stadium.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Chaotic_Bunimovich_stadium.png/440px-Chaotic_Bunimovich_stadium.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chaotic_Bunimovich_stadium.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Dynamical_billiards" title="Dynamical billiards">Billiards</a> on an oval <a href="/wiki/Billiard_table" title="Billiard table">billiard table</a>. Two particles, starting at the center with an angle differing by one degree, take paths that diverge chaotically because of <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(physics)" title="Reflection (physics)">reflections</a> at the boundary.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">Entropy</a> is broadly a measure of the disorder of some system. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statistical_thermodynamics" title="Statistical thermodynamics">statistical thermodynamics</a>, the entropy <i>S</i> of some physical system is defined as</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>S</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>k</mi>
<munder>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</munder>
<msub>
<mi>p</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<msub>
<mi>p</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>.</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle S=-k\sum _{i}p_{i}\ln(p_{i}).\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="S=-k\sum _{i}p_{i}\ln(p_{i}).\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4be67693caef12b846ed9cd173a0e7a340364d27" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:20.994ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The sum is over all possible states <i>i</i> of the system in question, such as the positions of gas particles in a container. Moreover, <span class="texhtml"><i>p</i><sub><i>i</i></sub></span> is the probability that the state <i>i</i> is attained and <i>k</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a>. Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Entropy_(information_theory)" title="Entropy (information theory)">entropy in information theory</a> measures the quantity of information. If a message recipient may expect any one of <i>N</i> possible messages with equal likelihood, then the amount of information conveyed by any one such message is quantified as <span class="texhtml">log<sub>2</sub>(<i>N</i>)</span> bits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lyapunov_exponent" title="Lyapunov exponent">Lyapunov exponents</a> use logarithms to gauge the degree of chaoticity of a <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_system" title="Dynamical system">dynamical system</a>. For example, for a particle moving on an oval billiard table, even small changes of the initial conditions result in very different paths of the particle. Such systems are <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">chaotic</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Deterministic_system" title="Deterministic system">deterministic</a> way, because small measurement errors of the initial state predictably lead to largely different final states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[86]</a></sup> At least one Lyapunov exponent of a deterministically chaotic system is positive.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fractals">Fractals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Fractals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sierpinski_dimension.svg"><img alt="Parts of a triangle are removed in an iterated way." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="108" data-file-width="745" height="58" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Sierpinski_dimension.svg/400px-Sierpinski_dimension.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Sierpinski_dimension.svg/600px-Sierpinski_dimension.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Sierpinski_dimension.svg/800px-Sierpinski_dimension.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sierpinski_dimension.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Sierpinski triangle (at the right) is constructed by repeatedly replacing <a href="/wiki/Equilateral_triangle" title="Equilateral triangle">equilateral triangles</a> by three smaller ones.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Logarithms occur in definitions of the <a href="/wiki/Fractal_dimension" title="Fractal dimension">dimension</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fractal" title="Fractal">fractals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[87]</a></sup> Fractals are geometric objects that are <a href="/wiki/Self-similarity" title="Self-similarity">self-similar</a>: small parts reproduce, at least roughly, the entire global structure. The <a href="/wiki/Sierpinski_triangle" title="Sierpinski triangle">Sierpinski triangle</a> (pictured) can be covered by three copies of itself, each having sides half the original length. This makes the <a href="/wiki/Hausdorff_dimension" title="Hausdorff dimension">Hausdorff dimension</a> of this structure <span class="texhtml">ln(3)/ln(2) ≈ 1.58</span>. Another logarithm-based notion of dimension is obtained by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Box-counting_dimension" title="Box-counting dimension">counting the number of boxes</a> needed to cover the fractal in question.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:354px;max-width:354px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:352px;max-width:352px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:4octavesAndfrequencies.jpg"><img alt="Four different octaves shown on a linear scale." data-file-height="96" data-file-width="568" height="59" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/4octavesAndfrequencies.jpg/350px-4octavesAndfrequencies.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/4octavesAndfrequencies.jpg/525px-4octavesAndfrequencies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ee/4octavesAndfrequencies.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:352px;max-width:352px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:4octavesAndfrequenciesEars.jpg"><img alt="Four different octaves shown on a logarithmic scale." data-file-height="96" data-file-width="577" height="58" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/4octavesAndfrequenciesEars.jpg/350px-4octavesAndfrequenciesEars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/4octavesAndfrequenciesEars.jpg/525px-4octavesAndfrequenciesEars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/4octavesAndfrequenciesEars.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Four different octaves shown on a linear scale, then shown on a logarithmic scale (as the ear hears them).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Logarithms are related to musical tones and <a href="/wiki/Interval_(music)" title="Interval (music)">intervals</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Equal_temperament" title="Equal temperament">equal temperament</a>, the frequency ratio depends only on the interval between two tones, not on the specific frequency, or <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(music)" title="Pitch (music)">pitch</a>, of the individual tones. For example, the <a href="/wiki/A_(musical_note)" title="A (musical note)">note <i>A</i></a> has a frequency of 440 <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">Hz</a> and <a href="/wiki/B%E2%99%AD_(musical_note)" title="B♭ (musical note)"><i>B-flat</i></a> has a frequency of 466 Hz. The interval between <i>A</i> and <i>B-flat</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Semitone" title="Semitone">semitone</a>, as is the one between <i>B-flat</i> and <a href="/wiki/B_(musical_note)" title="B (musical note)"><i>B</i></a> (frequency 493 Hz). Accordingly, the frequency ratios agree:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>466</mn>
<mn>440</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>≈<!-- ≈ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>493</mn>
<mn>466</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>≈<!-- ≈ --></mo>
<mn>1.059</mn>
<mo>≈<!-- ≈ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mroot>
<mn>2</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>12</mn>
</mrow>
</mroot>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {466}{440}}\approx {\frac {493}{466}}\approx 1.059\approx {\sqrt[{12}]{2}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\frac {466}{440}}\approx {\frac {493}{466}}\approx 1.059\approx {\sqrt[{12}]{2}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/55acf246da64ba711e1717eb43ad81792220ab32" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:27.175ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Therefore, logarithms can be used to describe the intervals: an interval is measured in semitones by taking the <span class="texhtml">base-2<sup>1/12</sup></span> logarithm of the <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a> ratio, while the <span class="texhtml">base-2<sup>1/1200</sup></span> logarithm of the frequency ratio expresses the interval in <a href="/wiki/Cent_(music)" title="Cent (music)">cents</a>, hundredths of a semitone. The latter is used for finer encoding, as it is needed for non-equal temperaments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[88]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; margin:1em auto 1em auto;">
<tr>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 100\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="100\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/66550d3448ba80a3f12cbb88eef69766cea6a356" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.906ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span></td>
<td><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
