<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_theory">Number theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Number theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">Natural logarithms</a> are closely linked to <a href="/wiki/Prime-counting_function" title="Prime-counting function">counting prime numbers</a> (2, 3, 5, 7, 11, ...), an important topic in <a href="/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a>. For any <a href="/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integer</a> <i>x</i>, the quantity of <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a> less than or equal to <i>x</i> is denoted <a href="/wiki/Prime-counting_function" title="Prime-counting function"><span class="texhtml"><span class="texhtml">π</span>(<i>x</i>)</span></a>. The <a href="/wiki/Prime_number_theorem" title="Prime number theorem">prime number theorem</a> asserts that <span class="texhtml"><span class="texhtml">π</span>(<i>x</i>)</span> is approximately given by</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {x}{\ln(x)}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\frac {x}{\ln(x)}}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3e7c35556de976b4896475a163d924bb9f3d83eb" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:6.624ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>in the sense that the ratio of <span class="texhtml"><span class="texhtml">π</span>(<i>x</i>)</span> and that fraction approaches 1 when <i>x</i> tends to infinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[89]</a></sup> As a consequence, the probability that a randomly chosen number between 1 and <i>x</i> is prime is inversely <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics)" title="Proportionality (mathematics)">proportional</a> to the number of decimal digits of <i>x</i>. A far better estimate of <span class="texhtml"><span class="texhtml">π</span>(<i>x</i>)</span> is given by the <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_integral_function" title="Logarithmic integral function">offset logarithmic integral</a> function <span class="texhtml">Li(<i>x</i>)</span>, defined by</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">L</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<msubsup>
<mo>∫<!-- ∫ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>x</mi>
</mrow>
</msubsup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mrow>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mi>d</mi>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathrm {Li} (x)=\int _{2}^{x}{\frac {1}{\ln(t)}}\,dt.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathrm {Li} (x)=\int _{2}^{x}{\frac {1}{\ln(t)}}\,dt." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/da577950b8b4c4ef726a3065afcdafa378dbc3fb" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:21.039ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Riemann_hypothesis" title="Riemann hypothesis">Riemann hypothesis</a>, one of the oldest open mathematical <a href="/wiki/Conjecture" title="Conjecture">conjectures</a>, can be stated in terms of comparing <span class="texhtml"><span class="texhtml">π</span>(<i>x</i>)</span> and <span class="texhtml">Li(<i>x</i>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[90]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Erd%C5%91s%E2%80%93Kac_theorem" title="Erdős–Kac theorem">Erdős–Kac theorem</a> describing the number of distinct <a href="/wiki/Prime_factor" title="Prime factor">prime factors</a> also involves the <a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">natural logarithm</a>.</p>
<p>The logarithm of <i>n</i> <a href="/wiki/Factorial" title="Factorial">factorial</a>, <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i>! = 1 · 2 · ... · <i>n</i></span>, is given by</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo>!</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>+</mo>
<mo>⋯<!-- ⋯ --></mo>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>.</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \ln(n!)=\ln(1)+\ln(2)+\cdots +\ln(n).\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\ln(n!)=\ln(1)+\ln(2)+\cdots +\ln(n).\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bf56cae097b659ddf6eff689c657b65dbd1472c3" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:36.415ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>This can be used to obtain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stirling%27s_formula" title="Stirling's formula">Stirling's formula</a>, an approximation of <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i>!</span> for large <i>n</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Generalizations">Generalizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Generalizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complex_logarithm">Complex logarithm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Complex logarithm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Complex_logarithm" title="Complex logarithm">Complex logarithm</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Complex_number_illustration_multiple_arguments.svg"><img alt="An illustration of the polar form: a point is described by an arrow or equivalently by its length and angle to the x axis." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="217" data-file-width="204" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Complex_number_illustration_multiple_arguments.svg/220px-Complex_number_illustration_multiple_arguments.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Complex_number_illustration_multiple_arguments.svg/330px-Complex_number_illustration_multiple_arguments.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Complex_number_illustration_multiple_arguments.svg/440px-Complex_number_illustration_multiple_arguments.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Complex_number_illustration_multiple_arguments.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Polar form of <span class="texhtml"><i>z = x + iy</i></span>. Both φ and φ' are arguments of <i>z</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex numbers</a> <i>a</i> solving the equation</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>e</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>a</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>z</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle e^{a}=z.\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="e^{a}=z.\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/46d7db1c5043c35ab5eac9e8effa27a1fa2887e1" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:7.455ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>are called <i>complex logarithms</i>. Here, <i>z</i> is a complex number. A complex number is commonly represented as <span class="texhtml"><i>z = x + iy</i></span>, where <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> are real numbers and <i>i</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Imaginary_unit" title="Imaginary unit">imaginary unit</a>. Such a number can be visualized by a point in the <a href="/wiki/Complex_plane" title="Complex plane">complex plane</a>, as shown at the right. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_form" title="Polar form">polar form</a> encodes a non-zero complex number <i>z</i> by its <a href="/wiki/Absolute_value" title="Absolute value">absolute value</a>, that is, the distance <i>r</i> to the <a href="/wiki/Origin_(mathematics)" title="Origin (mathematics)">origin</a>, and an angle between the <i>x</i> axis and the line passing through the origin and <i>z</i>. This angle is called the <a href="/wiki/Argument_(complex_analysis)" title="Argument (complex analysis)">argument</a> of <i>z</i>. The absolute value <i>r</i> of <i>z</i> is</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>r</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<msup>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<msup>
<mi>y</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle r={\sqrt {x^{2}+y^{2}}}.\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="r={\sqrt {x^{2}+y^{2}}}.\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0ab78ebf99b232388a968a6eb36d146158d9122a" style="vertical-align: -1.671ex; width:15.032ex; height:4.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The argument is not uniquely specified by <i>z</i>: both <span class="texhtml">φ</span> and <span class="texhtml">φ</span>' = <span class="texhtml">φ</span> + 2<span class="texhtml">π</span> are arguments of <i>z</i> because adding <span class="texhtml">2<span class="texhtml">π</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Radian" title="Radian">radians</a> or 360 degrees<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[nb 6]</a></sup> to φ corresponds to "winding" around the origin counter-clock-wise by a <a href="/wiki/Turn_(geometry)" title="Turn (geometry)">turn</a>. The resulting complex number is again <i>z</i>, as illustrated at the right. However, exactly one argument <span class="texhtml">φ</span> satisfies <span class="texhtml">−<span class="texhtml">π</span> &lt; φ</span> and <span class="texhtml">φ ≤ <span class="texhtml">π</span></span>. It is called the <i>principal argument</i>, denoted <span class="texhtml">Arg(<i>z</i>)</span>, with a capital <i>A</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup> (An alternative normalization is <span class="texhtml">0 ≤ Arg(<i>z</i>) &lt; 2<span class="texhtml">π</span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup>)</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Complex_log.jpg"><img alt="A density plot. In the middle there is a black point, at the negative axis the hue jumps sharply and evolves smoothly otherwise." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="636" data-file-width="636" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Complex_log.jpg/220px-Complex_log.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Complex_log.jpg/330px-Complex_log.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Complex_log.jpg/440px-Complex_log.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Complex_log.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The principal branch of the complex logarithm, Log(<i>z</i>). The black point at <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = 1</span> corresponds to absolute value zero and brighter (more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturation_(color_theory)" title="Saturation (color theory)">saturated</a>) colors refer to bigger absolute values. The <a href="/wiki/Hue" title="Hue">hue</a> of the color encodes the argument of Log(<i>z</i>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Using <a href="/wiki/Trigonometric_functions" title="Trigonometric functions">trigonometric functions</a> <a href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">sine</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosine" title="Cosine">cosine</a>, or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_exponential" title="Complex exponential">complex exponential</a>, respectively, <i>r</i> and φ are such that the following identities hold:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[94]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtable columnalign="left left left" columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mi>z</mi>
</mtd>
<mtd>
<mo>=</mo>
</mtd>
<mtd>
<mi>r</mi>
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mi>cos</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>φ<!-- φ --></mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>sin</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>φ<!-- φ --></mi>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
</mtd>
</mtr>
<mtr>
<mtd></mtd>
<mtd>
<mo>=</mo>
</mtd>
<mtd>
<mi>r</mi>
<msup>
<mi>e</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>φ<!-- φ --></mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>.</mo>
</mtd>
</mtr>
</mtable>
</mrow>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\begin{array}{lll}z&amp;=&amp;r\left(\cos \varphi +i\sin \varphi \right)\\&amp;=&amp;re^{i\varphi }.\end{array}}\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\begin{array}{lll}z&amp;=&amp;r\left(\cos \varphi +i\sin \varphi \right)\\&amp;=&amp;re^{i\varphi }.\end{array}}\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/633bd46125aaa6e5c6cd4e4aecf5d019890df710" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:25.893ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>This implies that the <span class="nowrap"><i>a</i>-th</span> power of <i>e</i> equals <i>z</i>, where</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>a</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>r</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>i</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>φ<!-- φ --></mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>n</mi>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>,</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a=\ln(r)+i(\varphi +2n\pi ),\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="a=\ln(r)+i(\varphi +2n\pi ),\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/75c163ea941c4074280b458ba35ef058af38229f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:24.039ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>φ is the principal argument Arg(<i>z</i>) and <i>n</i> is an arbitrary integer. Any such <i>a</i> is called a complex logarithm of <i>z</i>. There are infinitely many of them, in contrast to the uniquely defined real logarithm. If <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i> = 0</span>, <i>a</i> is called the <i>principal value</i> of the logarithm, denoted <span class="texhtml">Log(<i>z</i>)</span>. The principal argument of any positive real number <i>x</i> is 0; hence <span class="texhtml">Log(<i>x</i>)</span> is a real number and equals the real (natural) logarithm. However, the above formulas for logarithms of products and powers <a href="/wiki/Exponentiation#Failure_of_power_and_logarithm_identities" title="Exponentiation">do <i>not</i> generalize</a> to the principal value of the complex logarithm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>The illustration at the right depicts <span class="texhtml">Log(<i>z</i>)</span>. The discontinuity, that is, the jump in the hue at the negative part of the <i>x</i>- or real axis, is caused by the jump of the principal argument there. This locus is called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Branch_cut" title="Branch cut">branch cut</a>. This behavior can only be circumvented by dropping the range restriction on φ. Then the argument of <i>z</i> and, consequently, its logarithm become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-valued_function" title="Multi-valued function">multi-valued functions</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inverses_of_other_exponential_functions">Inverses of other exponential functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Inverses of other exponential functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Exponentiation occurs in many areas of mathematics and its inverse function is often referred to as the logarithm. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Logarithm_of_a_matrix" title="Logarithm of a matrix">logarithm of a matrix</a> is the (multi-valued) inverse function of the <a href="/wiki/Matrix_exponential" title="Matrix exponential">matrix exponential</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[96]</a></sup> Another example is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-adic_logarithm_function" title="P-adic logarithm function"><i>p</i>-adic logarithm</a>, the inverse function of the <a href="/wiki/P-adic_exponential_function" title="P-adic exponential function"><i>p</i>-adic exponential</a>. Both are defined via Taylor series analogous to the real case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup> In the context of <a href="/wiki/Differential_geometry" title="Differential geometry">differential geometry</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Exponential_map_(Riemannian_geometry)" title="Exponential map (Riemannian geometry)">exponential map</a> maps the <a href="/wiki/Tangent_space" title="Tangent space">tangent space</a> at a point of a <a href="/wiki/Differentiable_manifold" title="Differentiable manifold">manifold</a> to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neighborhood_(mathematics)" title="Neighborhood (mathematics)">neighborhood</a> of that point. Its inverse is also called the logarithmic (or log) map.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the context of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finite_groups" title="Finite groups">finite groups</a> exponentiation is given by repeatedly multiplying one group element <i>b</i> with itself. The <a href="/wiki/Discrete_logarithm" title="Discrete logarithm">discrete logarithm</a> is the integer <i>n</i> solving the equation</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>b</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle b^{n}=x,\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="b^{n}=x,\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/67eedbea39ea57a44cfbbfa0270dd69bf5dcf08f" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:7.727ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <i>x</i> is an element of the group. Carrying out the exponentiation can be done efficiently, but the discrete logarithm is believed to be very hard to calculate in some groups. This asymmetry has important applications in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_key_cryptography" title="Public key cryptography">public key cryptography</a>, such as for example in the <a href="/wiki/Diffie%E2%80%93Hellman_key_exchange" title="Diffie–Hellman key exchange">Diffie–Hellman key exchange</a>, a routine that allows secure exchanges of <a href="/wiki/Cryptography" title="Cryptography">cryptographic</a> keys over unsecured information channels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zech%27s_logarithm" title="Zech's logarithm">Zech's logarithm</a> is related to the discrete logarithm in the multiplicative group of non-zero elements of a <a href="/wiki/Finite_field" title="Finite field">finite field</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="double_logarithm"></span>Further logarithm-like inverse functions include the <i>double logarithm</i> ln(ln(<i>x</i>)), the <i><a href="/wiki/Super-logarithm" title="Super-logarithm">super- or hyper-4-logarithm</a></i> (a slight variation of which is called <a href="/wiki/Iterated_logarithm" title="Iterated logarithm">iterated logarithm</a> in computer science), the <a href="/wiki/Lambert_W_function" title="Lambert W function">Lambert W function</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Logit" title="Logit">logit</a>. They are the inverse functions of the <a href="/wiki/Double_exponential_function" title="Double exponential function">double exponential function</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tetration" title="Tetration">tetration</a>, of <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i>(<i>w</i>) = <i>we<sup>w</sup></i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[101]</a></sup> and of the <a href="/wiki/Logistic_function" title="Logistic function">logistic function</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_concepts">Related concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logarithm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Related concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From the perspective of <a href="/wiki/Group_theory" title="Group theory">group theory</a>, the identity <span class="texhtml">log(<i>cd</i>) = log(<i>c</i>) + log(<i>d</i>)</span> expresses a <a href="/wiki/Group_isomorphism" title="Group isomorphism">group isomorphism</a> between positive <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">reals</a> under multiplication and reals under addition. Logarithmic functions are the only continuous isomorphisms between these groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup> By means of that isomorphism, the <a href="/wiki/Haar_measure" title="Haar measure">Haar measure</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lebesgue_measure" title="Lebesgue measure">Lebesgue measure</a>) <i>dx</i> on the reals corresponds to the Haar measure <span class="texhtml"><i>dx</i>/<i>x</i></span> on the positive reals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">complex analysis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_geometry" title="Algebraic geometry">algebraic geometry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Differential_form" title="Differential form">differential forms</a> of the form <span class="texhtml">}<i>df</i>/<i>f</i></span> are known as forms with logarithmic <a href="/wiki/Pole_(complex_analysis)" title="Pole (complex analysis)">poles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Polylogarithm" title="Polylogarithm">polylogarithm</a> is the function defined by</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y mw-math-element" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>Li</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>s</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>z</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<munderover>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>k</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">∞<!-- ∞ --></mi>
</mrow>
</munderover>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<msup>
<mi>z</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>k</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<msup>
<mi>k</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>s</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \operatorname {Li} _{s}(z)=\sum _{k=1}^{\infty }{z^{k} \over k^{s}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\operatorname {Li} _{s}(z)=\sum _{k=1}^{\infty }{z^{k} \over k^{s}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c16a216f9168ba23df2d07ceb32c6929a70c4e1b" style="vertical-align: -3.171ex; width:16.647ex; height:7.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>It is related to the <a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">natural logarithm</a> by <span class="texhtml">Li<sub>1</sub>(<i>z</i>) = −ln(1 − <i>z</i>)</span>. Moreover, <span class="texhtml">Li<sub><i>s</i></sub>(1)</span> equals the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function" title="Riemann zeta function">Riemann zeta function</a> <span class="texhtml">ζ(<i>s</i>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-adaa-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-adaa_19-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some mathematicians disapprove of this notation. In his 1985 autobiography, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Halmos" title="Paul Halmos">Paul Halmos</a> criticized what he considered the "childish ln notation," which he said no mathematician had ever used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> The notation was invented by <a href="/wiki/Irving_Stringham" title="Irving Stringham">Irving Stringham</a>, a mathematician.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a>, <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haskell_(programming_language)" title="Haskell (programming language)">Haskell</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BASIC_programming_language" title="BASIC programming language">BASIC</a>.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The same series holds for the principal value of the complex logarithm for complex numbers <i>z</i> with positive real part.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-97">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a href="/wiki/Radian" title="Radian">radian</a> for the conversion between 2<a href="/wiki/Pi" title="Pi"><span class="texhtml">π</span></a> and 360 <a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">degrees</a>.</span></li>
